colspan=\"3\">\n<hr/>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td align=\"left\">c24.1</td>\n<td align=\"left\">64.39396249</td>\n<td align=\"left\">&minus;11.91067667</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td align=\"left\">c24.2</td>\n<td align=\"left\">64.39380000</td>\n<td align=\"left\">&minus;11.91072361</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<graphic xlink:href=\"apjab042bt4_tb.gif\"/>\n<graphic xlink:href=\"apjab042bt4_lr.gif\">\n<caption>\n<p>Typeset image</p>\n</caption>\n</graphic>\n</table-wrap>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs3-3\">\n<label>3.3.</label>\n<title>Mass Model Components</title>\n<p>As described in Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs3-1\">3.1</xref>, and as is typical for parametric lens modeling algorithms, the lens plane is described by a combination of several DM halos whose parameters are allowed to vary, with contribution from galaxy-scale halos that follow scaling relations. The lens model of MACS J0417 includes four &ldquo;free&rdquo; DM halos, all parameterized as PIEMDs. The dominant component is a cluster-scale halo, whose parameters are all allowed to vary, with the exception of the truncation radius <italic>r</italic><sub>cut</sub> that extends beyond the strong lensing regime and cannot be constrained by the strong lensing evidence. Three other halos are placed on the three BCGs, with positional parameters (<italic>x</italic>, <italic>y</italic>, <italic>&epsiv;</italic>, and <italic>&theta;</italic>) following their light distribution, and the other parameters set free. We emphasize that the halos placed on these galaxies are not to be considered strictly galaxy halos. The model cannot disentangle the DM halo in which the galaxy is embedded from the underlying DM halo of the cluster or group.</p>\n<p>Table <xref ref-type=\"table\" rid=\"apjab042bt3\">3</xref> lists the best-fit parameters of each halo, for several lens models. The &ldquo;Silver&rdquo; model uses as constraints the <italic>gold</italic> and <italic>silver</italic> arcs. The &ldquo;Bronze&rdquo; model uses <italic>gold</italic>, <italic>silver</italic>, and <italic>bronze</italic> constraints. We describe the third test model, labeled &ldquo;Bridge,&rdquo; below.</p>\n<p>As can be visually gleaned from the distribution of galaxies (Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf1\">1</xref>), the cluster core is fairly elongated, with the second and third brightest galaxies significantly separated in projection from the BCG. In the X-ray, Mann &amp; Ebeling (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib43\">2012</xref>) and Parekh et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib52\">2017</xref>) report extended emission elongated in the SE&ndash;NW direction. We, therefore, compute an additional lens model that includes a fifth PIEMD DM halo, forming a mass &ldquo;bridge&rdquo; between the central and NW components. We test this hypothesis using the <italic>gold</italic>&plus;<italic>silver</italic> list of constraints. The fifth halo is free to vary between the BCG and the NW component. The core radius of the potential is intentionally free to vary up to a high value (300 kpc) to allow a possible flat profile. The cut radius is fixed to a 1.5 Mpc as the main DM halo potential</p>\n<p>We quantitatively compare the quality of the three lens models using two criteria. The first one is the rms, which describes how well the model reproduces the image-plane positions of the constraints. The second one is the Bayesian Information Criterion (BIC; introduced by Schwarz <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib63\">1978</xref>), which is a statistical measurement based on the model likelihood <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${ \\mathcal L }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:mi mathvariant=\"italic\">&#x0E238;</mml:mi>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn94.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>, penalized by the number of free parameters <italic>k</italic> and the number of constraints <italic>n</italic>:<disp-formula id=\"apjab042beqn1\">\n<label>1</label>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA \\begin{eqnarray}\\mathrm{BIC}=-2\\times \\mathrm{log}({ \\mathcal L })+k\\times \\mathrm{log}(n).\\end{eqnarray}?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:mi>BIC</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo>&minus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>2</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mi>log</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mi mathvariant=\"italic\">&#x0E238;</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo>&plus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mi>k</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mi>log</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mi>n</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo>.</mml:mo>\n</mml:math> -->\n<graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042beqn1.gif\"/>\n</disp-formula>The rms gives a good indication of the global distance between the predicted image positions compared to the observed ones; thus for a fixed number of constraints a low rms generally implies a better model. The BIC quantifies an improvement in the model likelihood while taking into account a possible difference in the number of parameters and/or constraints between models. Thus a favorable model will be one with the best likelihood while keeping the lowest BIC value possible. Such criteria were used in previous analyses (Lagattuta et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib37\">2017</xref>; Jauzac et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib32\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2018</xref>; Mahler et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib42\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2018</xref>) to compare different variation models for a single cluster. The rms of the &ldquo;Bridge&rdquo; model is slightly better (0&farcs;36) compared to the fiducial model (0&farcs;37). However, the BIC shows an opposite trend when comparing the two models. We interpret a higher BIC value for the &ldquo;Bridge&rdquo; model as an over-fit of the model compared to a model without the bridge. In other words, the model does not improve enough to justify the addition of new parameters. Similar statistical analyses were made in other studies, e.g., using a discrimination by the evidence (Limousin et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib38\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2010</xref>), other likelihood penalization: Akaike Information Criterion (Acebron et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib1\">2017</xref>) or a combination of a large number of indicators (Jauzac et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib32\">2018</xref>).</p>\n<p>We compare the mass distribution between the models and plot their mass contours in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf2\">2</xref>. The difference between the two models is most notably the southeast region of the cluster. While the BCG area is well-constrained by systems surrounding the BCG, there is only one system with two images farther out. A confirmation of some of the lensed galaxy candidates with deeper observations would better constrain this region.</p>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf2\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 2.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc2\">\n<p>Cyan crosses show the position of all the individual DM potentials for our fiducial model. The top red cross shows the position of the center of the bridge potential. The red arrow indicates the shifted location of the main DM halo located at the red cross. The contour at 1.5&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;10<sup>9</sup><italic>M</italic><inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow/>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn95.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula> kpc<sup>&minus;2</sup> guides the eye to the apparent comet-like profile as seen in the X-ray luminosity distribution reported by previous studies (Ebeling et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib24\">2014</xref>; Parekh et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib52\">2017</xref>; Sandhu et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib62\">2018</xref>). A direct comparison between the DM and X-ray light distributions is shown and discussed in Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>). The projected mass density distributions are similar between the models in areas north of the BCG, and their contours are virtually indistinguishable around the BCG where the mass distribution is well constrained. The main differences between the models appear in the southeast, due to the lack of constraints in that side of the cluster (see Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs3-3\">3.3</xref> for more details).</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf2_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf2.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf2_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf2_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf2_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf2_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf3\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 3.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc3\">\n<p>Top: integrated mass profiles within a circular aperture centered on the BCG. Our parametric approach enables us to separate the different components of our mass profile. The profile labeled <italic>total</italic> represents our best fiducial model (i.e., using <italic>gold</italic> and <italic>silver</italic> constraints). The profile labeled DM represents the cluster-scale dark matter halo component (see Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs3-3\">3.3</xref>). The profiles labeled <italic>1stBCG</italic>, <italic>2ndBCG</italic>, and <italic>3rdBCG</italic> show the contribution of the three dark matter potentials placed at the locations of the three brightest galaxies of the cluster. The profile labeled <italic>cluster members</italic> represents the contribution of all cluster-member galaxies excluding the brightest three. We find a ratio between the main dark matter halo and the cluster members dark matter halo of about 100:1. Strong lensing constraints are plotted as vertical gray lines at their projected distance from the BCG. This is done to highlight where lensing constraints are available. In the regions where multiple images are not identified, the mass profile is an extrapolation. Weak lensing mass measurement from Applegate et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2\">2014</xref>) is plotted as a blue symbol. Bottom: mass density profiles as a function of the distance to the cluster center. The color coding follows the one from the top panel. The dark-red shaded areas show the 68&percnt;-confidence interval statistical uncertainty for the total mass profile, with the fractional error shown below each panel. We note that the small statistical uncertainties derived from the modeling underestimate the true error, which is driven by systematic uncertainties.</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf3_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf3.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf3_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf3_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf3_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf3_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n</sec>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs4\">\n<label>4.</label>\n<title>Discussion of Lens Model Results</title>\n<p>The spectroscopic capabilities of <italic>MUSE</italic> allow us to detect a central image for system&nbsp;1 buried in the light of the BCG. Our model predicts a radial pair at this location; however, only a single peak of emission is visible. We interpret that as the likely result of the source-plane caustic bisecting the galaxy in the source plane, resulting in a merging pair configuration where only a small fraction of the source galaxy is lensed into these positions. A more detailed analysis of the lensing configuration of this galaxy is presented in the companion paper by Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>).</p>\n<p>We report an effective Einstein radius of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${\\theta }_{E}\\simeq 36^{\\prime\\prime} $?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>&theta;</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>E</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&simeq;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>36</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo accent=\"false\">&Prime;</mml:mo>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn96.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>  for a source at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;9. The effective Einstein radius is the radius of a circle with the same area as an ellipse fitted to the critical curve. We measure a total projected mass of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${M}_{(200\\mathrm{kpc})}={1.78}_{-0.03}^{+0.01}\\,\\times {10}^{14}$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>200</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>kpc</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msubsup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>1.78</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&minus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.03</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&plus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.01</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msubsup>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>10</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>14</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msup>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn97.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula><italic>M</italic><inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow/>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn98.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula> within 200 kpc. Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf3\">3</xref> shows the radial mass profile centered on the BCG. Using the capability of our parametric approach we compute the mass profile of five different components of our cluster model: the main cluster-scale DM halo, the halos centered on the three BCGs, and the mass distribution of all the other galaxies, which follow a mass-to-light relation.</p>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf4\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 4.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc4\">\n<p>Redshift probability distribution functions (PDFs) of the multiply imaged galaxies used as constraints in the lensing analysis. The blue lines represent photometric redshift PDF estimates from <sc>bpz</sc> using the seven <italic>HST</italic> bands (dotted lines) and from <sc>eazy</sc> using the seven <italic>HST</italic> and two <italic>Spitzer</italic> bands (solid lines). The red shaded distributions are our lens model estimates based on MCMC sampling of the parameter space. The red vertical dashed lines show the best-fit value, model-<italic>z</italic>, for each system. The light orange shaded areas show predictions from the fiducial (&ldquo;silver&rdquo;) lens model for multiple images not included in the &ldquo;silver&rdquo; set: the <italic>bronze</italic> systems, 7 and 14, and system 10 with its third counter image 10.3 is included. Systems 1, 2, and 3 have a measured spectroscopic redshift shown as vertical black dashed lines. The dark gray shaded area marks the redshift range in front of the cluster (<italic>z</italic> &lt; 0.443). The light gray shaded area marks the redshift range 0.443&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;0.8, for which sources 4&ndash;17 would not be strongly lensed. The numbers in each panel correspond to the multiple image identification numbers as reported in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf1\">1</xref> and Table <xref ref-type=\"table\" rid=\"apjab042bt2\">2</xref>. An asterisk marks the <italic>bronze</italic> galaxies. See Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs4\">4</xref> for more detail.</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf4_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf4.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf4_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf4_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf4_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf4_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n<p>We qualitatively report a mass ratio of order 100:1 between the main cluster halo and the mass associated with the light of cluster elliptical galaxies, excluding the three brightest galaxies (dark green and magenta lines in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf3\">3</xref>). This is consistent with the relative mass to light ratio of rich clusters of about 10<sup>14</sup><italic>M</italic><inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow/>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn99.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula> as reported in Girardi et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib27\">2002</xref>). One will see that this qualitative result has no uncertainties attached, since the statistical uncertainties of the mass profile in Section <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf4\">4</xref> are likely underestimating the true uncertainty due to modeling assumptions (e.g., Meneghetti et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib45\">2017</xref> and structure along the line of sight; Chiriv&igrave; et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib16\">2018</xref>).</p>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs4-1\">\n<label>4.1.</label>\n<title>Photometric Redshifts</title>\n<p>The lens modeling procedure left the redshift of multiply imaged systems with no spectroscopic confirmation free to vary. It explores the parameter space to find the most likely redshift (model-<italic>z</italic>) of each system. Generally, we find that the redshifts predicted by the &ldquo;Silver&rdquo; model are in agreement with those predicted by the &ldquo;Bronze&rdquo; model. However, a comparison between the lens model-predicted redshifts (model-<italic>z</italic>) and photometric redshift (photo-<italic>z</italic>) estimates can be used for a qualitative assessment of the validity of the lens model.</p>\n<p>The RELICS program delivered photometric redshift catalogs using <sc>bpz</sc> (Ben&iacute;tez <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib8\">2000</xref>; Coe et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib18\">2006</xref>) based on <italic>HST</italic> photometry measured in ACS and WFC3 images. We compare our model-<italic>z</italic> results against photometric redshifts from the public catalog, as well as against a photometric redshift analysis that supplements the <italic>HST</italic> data with <italic>Spitzer</italic> photometry and uses a different algorithm, <sc>eazy</sc> (Brammer et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib13\">2008</xref>). A thorough description of the <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer z</italic><sub>phot</sub> analysis will be provided in a forthcoming paper (Strait et al. 2019, in preparation).</p>\n<p>The multiplicity (i.e., having multiple images for each lensed source) provides an additional means to test the robustness of the photometric redshifts of the lensed galaxies in this field. We note that in some cases, the photo-<italic>z</italic> measured for the different multiple images of the same source can disagree. That happens even for systems where the visual identification of the multiple images is entirely unambiguous. This discrepancy could be due to contamination from nearby sources (usually cluster members); variations in SExtractor&rsquo;s detection, deblending, and segmentation for each multiple image; and photo-<italic>z</italic> degeneracies, especially when the lensed image is very faint (mag &gt; 28). Structure along the line of sight could potentially increase the uncertainty of the model-<italic>z</italic>, and contribute to a discrepancy between model-<italic>z</italic> and photo-<italic>z</italic>. However, this effect is likely not significant, and not the main source of discrepancy (Chiriv&igrave; et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib16\">2018</xref>).</p>\n<p>For these reasons, it is instructive to examine the entire probability distribution function (PDF) of the photo-<italic>z</italic> and model-<italic>z</italic> when assessing the agreement between them. We show them in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf4\">4</xref>.</p>\n<p>Ruling out photo-<italic>z</italic> solutions that place securely identified lensed galaxies in front of the cluster, we find that the model-<italic>z</italic> PDF of most of the sources are in good agreement with the photo-<italic>z</italic> PDF of at least one of the multiple images of that source. However, we note a discrepancy between the model-<italic>z</italic> and the photo-<italic>z</italic> in the case of some of the sources and discuss it in the following paragraph.</p>\n<p>The most problematic discrepancy is for source&nbsp;9. The <italic>HST</italic> colors and both the <italic>HST</italic> and <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer z</italic><sub>phot</sub> PDFs rule out redshifts above 6, and the photo-<italic>z</italic> solutions of the two different images of the same source are in agreement. However, when the redshift of this system is set as a free parameter with a flat prior and no upper limit, all the lens models, including the &ldquo;bridge&rdquo; model, favor an extremely high redshift (<italic>z</italic> &sim; 9), albeit with large uncertainty. System&nbsp;9 is a pair of images that closely straddle the critical curve. Such systems, if their spectroscopic redshift is known, can be excellent constraints, since they tightly constrain the location of the critical curve. On the other hand, when the redshift of such a pair is unknown, only the position of the critical curve is constrained but not its redshift. Based on the colors and photo-<italic>z</italic> estimates for this source, we rule out the <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;9 solution. To examine the effect of this wrong solution on the lens model results, we computed a separate model with the redshift of system&nbsp;9 fixed at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;5.75, the most probable photo-<italic>z</italic> of image 9.1 from the <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> <sc>eazy</sc> photo-<italic>z</italic> analysis.<xref ref-type=\"fn\" rid=\"apjab042bfn5\"><sup>26</sup></xref>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bfn5\">\n<label><sup>26</sup></label>\n<p>The <italic>HST</italic> <sc>bpz</sc> analysis yields <italic>z</italic><sub>phot</sub> &sim; 5.4, thus this galaxy was not included as a high-<italic>z</italic> candidate in Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>).</p>\n</fn> The outputs of the resulting model are not significantly different from models that leave this parameter free. Motivated by this examination, in our final model, we set the upper limit of the redshift of system&nbsp;9 to <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&le;&nbsp;6.</p>\n<p>The model predicted redshifts of sources 14 and 16 show a discrepancy with the <italic>HST</italic> <sc>bpz</sc> PDF; however, the <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> photo-<italic>z</italic> increases the likelihood at higher redshifts, and their probability distributions do not rule out the model-<italic>z</italic>. Moreover, system 14 is faint (mag &sim; 28&ndash;29) and classified as <italic>bronze</italic>, making this disagreement less concerning.</p>\n<p>For source 7, both photo-<italic>z</italic> analyses favor higher redshift solutions for this source, <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;3.5, while the model-<italic>z</italic> converges to <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;2.2. The region in which this source appears is well constrained by images of sources&nbsp;12 and 13. For 7.1 and 7.2 to be multiple images of the same source, source&nbsp;7 must be at lower redshift than sources&nbsp;12 and 13. If the photo-<italic>z</italic> is correct, this source may be misidentified, as already suggested by its classification as <italic>bronze</italic>.</p>\n<p>The photo-<italic>z</italic> PDFs of several systems, including systems 8 and 17 on the opposite side of systemas 9 and 14, indicate several solutions spanning a large range. Some of these solutions favor a higher redshift than predicted by the lens model. However, we cannot make definitive conclusions for such systems.</p>\n<p>Finally, we note that the photometric redshifts that were estimated from the <italic>HST</italic> data alone were calculated using the <sc>bpz</sc> algorithm, and <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> photometric redshifts were calculated with <sc>eazy</sc>. While a thorough comparison of photometric redshifts is beyond the scope of this paper (e.g., Salmon et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>), we show in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf8\">8</xref> in Appendix <xref ref-type=\"app\" rid=\"apjab042bapp2\">B</xref> a similar comparison using the <sc>eazy</sc> algorithm for both the <italic>HST</italic> and <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> photometric redshifts.</p>\n</sec>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs5\">\n<label>5.</label>\n<title>High-redshift Predictions</title>\n<p>During the first year of <italic>JWST</italic> science operations, at least 13 galaxy clusters will be observed in the context of the Guaranteed Time Observations (GTO) and <italic>Director</italic>&rsquo;<italic>s Discretionary Early Release Science</italic> (DD-ERS) programs (PIs: Windhorst, Willott, Stiavelli, Rigby, and Treu) using all four <italic>JWST</italic> instruments: the Near-Infrared Camera (NIRCam), the Near-Infrared Imager and Slitless Spectrograph (NIRISS), Near-Infrared Spectrograph (NIRSpec), and the Mid-Infrared Instrument (MIRI). These observations will include NIRCam imaging to various depths for all 13 clusters. MACS J0417.5&minus;1154 is included in this list of 13 clusters and will be observed thanks to the Canadian NIRISS Unbiased Cluster Survey GTO program (CANUCS; PI: Willott).</p>\n<p>We use our lens model and UV luminosity functions from Mason et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib44\">2015</xref>) to predict numbers of objects observable by <italic>JWST</italic> at 8&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;16, before and during the epoch of reionization. We also explore and discuss the expectations from the <italic>HST</italic> RELICS observations that yielded 321 candidates with photometric redshifts <italic>z</italic><sub>phot</sub>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;8 in 46 cluster fields, but none from this cluster (Salmon et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>).</p>\n<p>Observing the high-redshift universe behind a cluster offers a boost in sensitivity to lower luminosities, but diminishes the FOV. In Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf5\">5</xref>, we demonstrate how the effective observed FOV of 2&farcm;2&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;2&farcm;2 (4.8 arcmin<sup>2</sup>, or one of the two modules observed by the <italic>JWST</italic>/ NIRCam), is affected by gravitational lensing. The magnification map for a source at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;16 is ray-traced through the best-fit model to the source plane. This transformation reveals the spatial extent of the background area covered by such an observation, resulting in an unlensed observed high-<italic>z</italic> area of 1.3 arcmin<sup>2</sup>.</p>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf5\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 5.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc5\">\n<p>Delensed image of the MACS J0417 magnification map for sources at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;16, showing the source-plane area (1.3 arcmin<sup>2</sup>) lensed into a 2&farcm;2&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;2&farcm;2 field of view (4.8 arcmin<sup>2</sup>), field of view covered by a single NIRCam module. The color scale shows the magnification factor in magnitudes. Beyond <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;7, the delensed map does not differ significantly from the one presented here.</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf5_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf5.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf5_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf5_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf5_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf5_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n<p>Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf6\">6</xref> shows the expected cumulative number counts (not accounting for incompleteness) for MACS J0417, or a galaxy cluster with similar lensing strength, as a function of magnitude, for galaxies at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;6, 8, 10, 12, 14, and 16 within the FOV of a single NIRCam module (roughly aligned with the WFC3/IR FOV). We adopt blank field luminosity functions from Mason et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib44\">2015</xref>) due to its ability to predict density at any redshift. The faint-end slope of this luminosity function increases from <italic>&agr;</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;&minus;2.1 at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;8 to <italic>&agr;</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;&minus;3.5 at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;16. Such steep faint-end slopes would imply that many small, faint galaxies are magnified into view by lensing and that there is a significant efficiency gain from strong lensing to discover the first galaxies with <italic>JWST</italic>.</p>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf6\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 6.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc6\">\n<p>Cumulative number counts (not accounting for incompleteness) of galaxies expected at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6, 8, 10, 12, 14, and 16 in a 5 arcmin<sup>2</sup> blank field (dashed lines) and lensed field (solid lines) based on luminosity functions from Mason et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib44\">2015</xref>) and our lens model of MACS J0417. The black line very roughly assumes that a 1 Ms program could detect galaxies with AB mag 32.2 in a single deep field and that the flux limit scales with <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA $\\sqrt{\\mathrm{exposure}\\ \\mathrm{time}}$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msqrt>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>exposure</mml:mi>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.33em\"/>\n<mml:mi>time</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msqrt>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn100.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula> if that 1 Ms is spread across a larger area. We expect strong lensing clusters such as these to deliver significant efficiency gains in discovering the first galaxies with <italic>JWST</italic>, especially if luminosity function faint-end slopes are as steep as predicted by Mason et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib44\">2015</xref>).</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf6_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf6.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf6_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf6_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf6_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf6_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n<p>Cluster observations scheduled for the first year of <italic>JWST</italic> will typically reach magnitudes of &sim;29 AB and fainter. From Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf6\">6</xref>, we expect that at this magnitude limits this field hosts three lensed galaxies at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;10, and less than one galaxy in each of the higher redshift bins, not accounting for detection efficiency and incompleteness. Observing an order of a dozen clusters should yield galaxies as distant as <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;12 and a substantial sample of high-<italic>z</italic> galaxies at the epoch of reionization.</p>\n<p>In Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf7\">7</xref>, we compare the lensing strength of MACS J0417 to other clusters from the RELICS program for which lens models are publicly available on the MAST, including those published by Cerny et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib15\">2018</xref>); Acebron et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2a\">2018a</xref>, <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2b\">2018b</xref>); Cibirka et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib17\">2018</xref>), and Paterno-Mahler et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib53\">2018</xref>). The previous version of MACS J0417 lens model, V1, available on the MAST, predicts &sim;20&percnt; higher number counts for relatively bright sources (AB mag 25), and similar number counts for faint sources, giving an indication of the systematic uncertainties due to spectroscopic redshift availability, and different modeling assumptions.</p>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf7\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 7.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc7\">\n<p>Expected number counts (not accounting for incompleteness) of <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;6 galaxies in blank fields (dashed line) or lensed by RELICS clusters according to our models (solid lines). The first RELICS lens model (V1; dark blue line) predicts MACS J0417 to have a relatively average lensing strength compared to other RELICS clusters. On the other hand, the model presented in this paper (V2; black line) is among the 25&percnt; weakest of 21 RELICS clusters for which mass models are already available. All expectations are scaled to the full area of 213 arcmin<sup>2</sup> covered on the sky by RELICS. The publicly available lens models were derived with various methods: <monospace>Lenstool</monospace> (Kneib et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib36\">1996</xref>; Jullo et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib35\">2007</xref>), Zitrin-LTM (Broadhurst et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib14\">2005</xref>; Zitrin et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib68\">2015</xref>), and GLAFIC (Oguri <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib50\">2010</xref>).</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf7_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf7.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf7_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf7_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf7_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf7_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n<p>With the updated model (V2), we find that MACS J0417 is ranked in the lower 25th percentile of these clusters when it comes to the lensing strength. However, as other RELICS clusters, MACS0417 is among the most powerful lenses known to date.</p>\n<p>In a photometric search for <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;8 galaxies in the entire RELICS survey, Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>) report 321 candidates, with a median of six candidates per field and an average of seven, none of which are in the field of MACS J0417. From Poisson statistics alone, there is a 4&percnt; chance that at least 1 of the 46 RELICS fields would yield no <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;8 candidates, given the average of seven per field. Cosmic variance would increase this likelihood somewhat (Trenti &amp; Stiavelli <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib64\">2008</xref>), especially in a lensed field (Robertson et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib59\">2014</xref>). However, our lensing analysis indicates that the lensing strength of MACS J0417 is not extraordinarily low compared to other RELICS clusters for which models are available. It is therefore odd that Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>) detected no <italic>z</italic><sub>phot</sub>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;8 candidates in this field.</p>\n<p>Quantitatively, the prediction for MACS J0417, shown in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf7\">7</xref>, indicates that this field should host about 5.34 <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6 magnified galaxies at, or brighter than, 27 mag. The actual expected number would be lower, due to incompleteness. A thorough investigation, including completeness estimates, is required (e.g., Livermore et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib41\">2017</xref>). However, we can get a rough estimate of the detection efficiency of Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>) for the discovery of <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6 galaxies from their actual detection histograms. Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>) discovered 211 candidates with F160W AB mag &le;27 in the <italic>z</italic><sub>phot</sub>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;6 bin in all of the RELICS fields. From Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf7\">7</xref>, we expect there to be at most 300 galaxies at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;6 with observed AB magnitude below 27 within the same observed area. A comparison of the number of candidates observed with the predicted number implies an estimated <italic>average</italic> efficiency of at least 70&percnt;. Assuming this efficiency, we would have expected Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>) to find at least 5.34&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;70&percnt;&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;3.74 galaxies in this range behind MACS J0417. Assuming small number statistics, the zero detection is discrepant with this estimated expectation (for example, Poisson statistics would give a range of 1&ndash;8 at 95&percnt; confidence level). The low number of candidates in this field could be a result of lower-than-average density of galaxies at this location due to cosmic variance. However, such discrepancy suggests a reanalysis of this particular field is needed.</p>\n<p>As can be seen in Figures <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf3\">3</xref> and <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf8\">8</xref>, some of the <sc>eazy</sc> photo-<italic>z</italic> PDFs favor <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;5.5 solutions for some of the multiple images. A preliminary <sc>bpz</sc> reanalysis of this field puts source&nbsp;9 slightly above <italic>z</italic><sub>phot</sub>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;5.5, which would increase the number of candidates in this field to two <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6 candidates. Therefore, reducing the disagreement between predictions and detections.</p>\n<fig id=\"apjab042bf8\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Figure 8.</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042bfc8\">\n<p>Same as Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf4\">4</xref>, but here both the <italic>HST</italic>-only and <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> PDFs are computed with the same algorithm, EAZY. While there are some differences between the EASY and BPZ outputs, choosing one algorithm over the other does not change the results of this paper.</p>\n</caption>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf8_eps\" content-type=\"print\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf8.eps\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf8_lr\" content-type=\"low\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf8_lr.jpg\"/>\n<graphic id=\"apjab042bf8_hr\" content-type=\"high\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bf8_hr.jpg\"/>\n</fig>\n<p>An analysis of this field and all RELICS fields based on the combined <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> photometry is in progress (V. Strait et al. 2019, in preparation). Adding the <italic>Spitzer</italic> photometry could remove some of the degeneracies and improve the photometric redshift estimates.</p>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs6\">\n<label>6.</label>\n<title>Discussion and Summary</title>\n<p>We present a strong lens model of MACS J0417.5&minus;1154, updating the model previously released by the RELICS collaboration. This cluster was selected by the RELICS program for its promising lensing capabilities. We identified 57 multiple images belonging to 17 lensed background sources. We also report lensing candidates that were not reliable enough to be used as constraints but are nevertheless of potential interest for further study by current or upcoming facilities such as <italic>JWST</italic>. This study and the companion paper by Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>) represent the first published strong lensing analyses of this cluster.</p>\n<p>Our strong lensing analysis compares models based on constraints with different levels of reliability (<italic>silver</italic> and <italic>bronze</italic>) as well as different levels of complexity of the lens plane, for example when including a bridge of matter between the two main substructures of the cluster. Our analysis reveals that the addition of a <italic>bridge</italic> potential, while giving a lower rms does not satisfy our BIC criteria. Therefore, we keep a fiducial model constrained by our <italic>silver</italic> sample with no potential acting as a bridge of matter between substructures of the cluster.</p>\n<p>From our strong lensing mass modeling, we measure a total projected mass within 200 kpc of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${M}_{(200\\mathrm{kpc})}={1.78}_{-0.03}^{+0.01}\\,\\times {10}^{14}$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>200</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>kpc</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msubsup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>1.78</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&minus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.03</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&plus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.01</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msubsup>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>10</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>14</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msup>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn101.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula><italic>M</italic><inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow/>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn102.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>. Despite the limited ability of strong lens models to measure the mass beyond the multiple image region, this value is within 3<italic>&sgr;</italic> of the mass <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${M}_{1.5\\mathrm{Mpc}}\\,=(18.9\\pm 0.25)\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>1.5</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>Mpc</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>18.9</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo>&PlusMinus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.25</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>10</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>14</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msup>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn105.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula> measured by weak lensing analysis (Applegate et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2\">2014</xref>). We report for this cluster an Einstein radius of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${\\theta }_{E}\\simeq 36^{\\prime\\prime} $?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>&theta;</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>E</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&simeq;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>36</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo accent=\"false\">&Prime;</mml:mo>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn106.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>  at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;9. Using the parameters of the spherical Navarro&ndash;Frenk&ndash;White (NFW; Navarro et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib4a\">1997</xref>) profile fitted in Applegate et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2\">2014</xref>), we derived an Einstein radius at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;9 of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${\\theta }_{{E}_{\\mathrm{NFW}}}\\simeq 26^{\\prime\\prime} $?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>&theta;</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>E</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>NFW</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&simeq;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>26</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo accent=\"false\">&Prime;</mml:mo>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn107.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>. The large mass reported by Applegate et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2\">2014</xref>) still provides a reasonably close Einstein radius compare to our analysis. In addition, strong lensing analysis of CLASH clusters (Zitrin et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib68\">2015</xref>) report, for comparable clusters, similar values.</p>\n<p>We examine the agreement between photo-<italic>z</italic> and model-<italic>z</italic> for the sample of multiple images selected in our study. There is a general agreement when the low-<italic>z</italic> solutions for the photo-<italic>z</italic> are excluded. System 7 might be a misidentification. The agreements for systems 12 and 13 benefit from the reduced redshift range during the optimization of system 9 induced by the initial disagreement with photo-<italic>z</italic>. A detailed study of the influence of the photometric redshift algorithm or the data set is beyond the scope of this paper as this would need more spectroscopic redshifts to be used as a benchmark to remove biases in the comparison.</p>\n<p>Our previous model of MACS J0417 suggested its lensing strength was about average among all RELICS clusters modeled to date (all of which are powerful lenses). The new lens model presented here suggests MACS J0417 is in the lower 25th percentile of the RELICS clusters. Still the lack of any <italic>z</italic><sub>phot</sub>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;8 candidates in this field is at odds with the expected number, estimated from the lensing magnification of this field, assumptions on the high-<italic>z</italic> luminosity functions, and our estimate of the average detection efficiency of Salmon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>). We primarily attribute this to cosmic variance, but we will reanalyze this field and perform completeness simulations to determine if there are some other reasons besides cosmic variance for the low yield of high-<italic>z</italic> candidates. MACS J0417 is still expected to be an efficient lens for upcoming <italic>JWST</italic> GTO observations to discover fainter and higher redshift candidates. Strong lensing clusters will continue to deliver significant efficiency gains toward discovering high-redshift galaxies and the first galaxies with <italic>JWST</italic>.</p>\n</sec> </body>\n<back>\n<ack>\n<p>Support for program GO-14096 was provided by NASA through a grant from the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., under NASA contract NAS5-26555. These galaxies were not deemed reliable enough to be used as constraints. If confirmed with deeper observations, they could become useful lensing evidence to constrain areas in the field that are currently under-constrained. Table <xref ref-type=\"table\" rid=\"apjab042bt4\">4</xref> lists the candidate IDs and coordinates. They are plotted in Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf1\">1</xref>.</p>\n</app>\n<app id=\"apjab042bapp2\">\n<label>Appendix B</label>\n<title>EASY Photo-<italic>z</italic> Estimates</title>\n<p>The photo-<italic>z</italic> estimates that were used in this analysis are computed with two different algorithms, the <italic>HST</italic>-only analysis was done with BPZ and matches the catalogs that are publicly available on MAST. The <italic>HST</italic>&plus;<italic>Spitzer</italic> analysis uses EAZY. In this appendix, we repeat the comparison between model-predicted redshift PDFs and those of the photo-<italic>z</italic> estimates, using EASY for both sets of photo-<italic>z</italic> measurements (see Figure <xref ref-type=\"fig\" rid=\"apjab042bf8\">8</xref>). The same <italic>HST</italic> photometric catalogs, also available on MAST, were used in all cases. We find that the choice of photo-<italic>z</italic> algorithm does not significantly change our conclusion that the photometric redshifts and model redshifts are generally in good agreement.</p>\n</app>\n</app-group>\n<ref-list content-type=\"alphabetical\">\n<title>References</title>\n<ref id=\"apjab042bbib2b\">\n<element-citation publication-type=\"journal\">\n<person-group al.</p>\n</author-comment>\n</contrib-group>\n<pub-date pub-type=\"ppub\">\n<day>1</day>\n<month>3</month>\n<year>2019</year>\n</pub-date>\n<pub-date pub-type=\"epub\">\n<day>07</day>\n<month>03</month>\n<year>2019</year>\n</pub-date>\n<volume>873</volume>\n<issue>1</issue>\n<elocation-id content-type=\"artnum\">96</elocation-id>\n<history>\n<date date-type=\"received\">\n<day>6</day>\n<month>10</month>\n<year>2018</year>\n</date>\n<date date-type=\"rev-recd\">\n<day>28</day>\n<month>1</month>\n<year>2019</year>\n</date>\n<date date-type=\"accepted\">\n<day>31</day>\n<month>1</month>\n<year>2019</year>\n</date>\n</history>\n<permissions>\n<copyright-statement>&copy; 2019. The American Astronomical Society. All rights reserved.</copyright-statement>\n<copyright-year>2019</copyright-year>\n</permissions>\n<self-uri xlink:href=\"http://stacks.iop.org/apj/873/96\"/>\n<abstract>\n<title>Abstract</title>\n<p>Strong gravitational lensing by clusters of galaxies probes the mass distribution at the core of each cluster and magnifies the universe behind it. MACS J0417.5&minus;1154 at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;0.443 is one of the most massive clusters known based on weak lensing, X-ray, and Sunyaev&ndash;Zel&rsquo;dovich analyses. Here we compute a strong lens model of MACS J0417&nbsp;based on <italic>Hubble Space Telescope</italic> imaging observations collected, in part, by the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS), and recently reported spectroscopic redshifts from the <italic>MUSE</italic> instrument on the Very Large Telescope (VLT). We measure an Einstein radius of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${\\theta }_{E}\\simeq 36^{\\prime\\prime} $?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>&theta;</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>E</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&simeq;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>36</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo accent=\"false\">&Prime;</mml:mo>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn1.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>  at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;9 and a mass projected within 200 kpc of <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${M}_{(200\\mathrm{kpc})}={1.78}_{-0.03}^{+0.01}\\times {10}^{14}$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>200</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>kpc</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msubsup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>1.78</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&minus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.03</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&plus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.01</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msubsup>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>10</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>14</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msup>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn2.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula><italic>M</italic><inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow/>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn3.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>. Using this model, we measure a ratio between the mass attributed to cluster-member galaxy halos and the main cluster halo of order 1:100. We assess the probability to detect magnified high-redshift galaxies in the field of this cluster, both for comparison with RELICS <italic>HST</italic> results and as a prediction for the <italic>James Webb Space Telescope</italic> (<italic>JWST</italic>)&nbsp;Guaranteed Time Observations upcoming for this cluster. Our lensing analysis indicates that this cluster has similar lensing strength to other clusters in the RELICS program. Our lensing analysis predicts a detection of at least a few <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;8 galaxies behind this cluster, at odds with a recent analysis that yielded no such candidates in this field. Reliable strong lensing models are crucial for accurately predicting the intrinsic properties of lensed galaxies. As part of the RELICS program, our strong lensing model produced with the <monospace>Lenstool</monospace> parametric method is publicly available through the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes.</p>\n</abstract>\n<kwd-group kwd-group-type=\"AAS\">\n<kwd>galaxies: clusters: individual (MACSJ0417.5&ndash;1154)</kwd>\n<kwd>gravitational lensing: strong</kwd>\n</kwd-group>\n<counts>\n<page-count count=\"15\"/>\n</counts>\n<custom-meta-group>\n<custom-meta>\n<meta-name>ccc</meta-name>\n<meta-value>0004-637X/19/96&plus;15&dollar;33.00</meta-value>\n</custom-meta>\n<custom-meta>\n<meta-name>crossmark</meta-name>\n<meta-value>yes</meta-value>\n</custom-meta>\n</custom-meta-group>\n</article-meta>\n</front>\n<body>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs1\">\n<label>1.</label>\n<title>Introduction</title>\n<p>In our view of the history of the universe, the epoch of reionization remains the least well observed. During the first billion years, the universe was largely neutral. Half the intergalactic medium (IGM) in the universe was reionized by <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;8&nbsp;&PlusMinus;&nbsp;1 (Planck Collaboration et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib54\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2016a</xref>) and nearly completely by <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;6. The end of reionization is evidenced by Gunn &amp; Peterson (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib30\">1965</xref>) troughs (due to absorption by neutral intergalactic hydrogen) observed in <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;6 quasar spectra, but not in spectra at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;6 (Becker et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib7\">2001</xref>, <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib6\">2015</xref>; Djorgovski et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib20\">2001</xref>; Fan et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib26\">2006</xref>). Observing galaxies during the epoch of reionization remains a challenge today. They are much fainter due to their great distance and smaller sizes, and any Ly<italic>&agr;</italic> emission is often scattered or absorbed by the surrounding neutral gas.</p>\n<p>Strong lensing magnification by clusters of galaxies offers a privileged view of the high-<italic>z</italic> universe. Several studies already highlight the high power of gravitational lenses to reveal objects that would have been inaccessible otherwise. Deep observations of Frontier Fields clusters (Lotz et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib1a\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2017</xref>) were particularly important for probing the faint end of high-redshift luminosity functions and the galaxies most likely responsible for reionization (Atek et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib3\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2015</xref>, <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib4\">2018</xref>; Livermore et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib41\">2017</xref>; Bouwens et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib11\">2017</xref>; Yue et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib66\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2018</xref>; Bhatawdekar et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib10\">2018</xref>; Ishigaki et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib31\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2018</xref>), as well as finding high-redshift candidates (e.g., a <italic>z</italic> &sim; 10 galaxy from Oesch et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib49\">2018</xref>). The Cluster Lensing And Supernova survey with Hubble (CLASH; Postman et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib57\">2012</xref>) yielded <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&sim;&nbsp;6&ndash;11 galaxies to be observed more brightly (Hashimoto et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib1b\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2018</xref>; Zheng et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib67\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2012</xref>; Coe et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib19\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2013</xref>; Bradley et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib12\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2014</xref>). Even after these large surveys, many clusters had yet to be observed by the <italic>Hubble Space Telescope (HST)</italic> at near-infrared wavelengths (1.0&ndash;1.7 <italic>&mgr;</italic>m) to search for distant galaxies.</p>\n<p>MACS J0417.5&minus;1154 (hereafter, MACS J0417) was discovered by the MAssive Cluster Survey (MACS; Ebeling et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib23\">2001</xref>) as part of the <italic>ROSAT</italic> (Voges et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib65\">1999</xref>) catalog of bright sources. MACS J0417 at <italic>z</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;0.443 is one of the most X-ray luminous clusters with a luminosity of 2.9&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;10<sup>45</sup> erg s<sup>&minus;1</sup> between 0.1 and 2.4 keV. Based on <italic>Chandra</italic> X-ray observations, Mann &amp; Ebeling (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib43\">2012</xref>) report that the peak of the X-ray emission is centered on the primary brightest cluster galaxy (BCG) with a slight diffuse emission extended toward the second brightest galaxy in the cluster core. Dwarakanath et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib22\">2011</xref>), Parekh et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib52\">2017</xref>), and Sandhu et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib62\">2018</xref>) confirm this feature in the radio. Parekh et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib52\">2017</xref>) highlight the similarity in morphology to the clusters A2746 and 1E&nbsp;0657&minus;56 (the &ldquo;Bullet cluster&rdquo;), strengthening the hypothesis made by Mann &amp; Ebeling (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib43\">2012</xref>) that MACS J0417 is a recent merger, probably oriented along the line of sight, or alternatively, caught close to a turnaround. The merging state of MACS J0417 is also confirmed in the analysis of Pandge et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib1d\">2018</xref>). Recently, Botteon et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib1e\">2018</xref>) discovered two new cold fronts indicating that substructure dynamics are at play in MACS J0417.</p>\n<p>MACS J0417 was also detected by the <italic>Planck</italic> Early Sunyaev&ndash;Zel&rsquo;dovich (ESZ) catalog (Planck Collaboration et al. <xref rid=\"apjab042bbib55\" ref-type=\"bibr\">2011</xref>), and with <inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${M}_{500}=(1.23\\pm 0.05)\\times {10}^{15}{M}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>500</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>1.23</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo>&PlusMinus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.05</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>10</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>15</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msup>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn4.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula> had the fourth highest mass of all 1094 confirmed clusters with measured redshifts and mass estimates in the <italic>Planck</italic> PSZ2 catalog (Planck Collaboration et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib56\">2016b</xref>). Similarly, MACS J0417 has the third-highest mass (<inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA ${M}_{1500\\mathrm{kpc}}=(1.89\\pm 0.25)\\times {10}^{15}{M}_{\\odot }$?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>1500</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>kpc</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n<mml:mo>&equals;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">(</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>1.89</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo>&PlusMinus;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>0.25</mml:mn>\n<mml:mo stretchy=\"false\">)</mml:mo>\n<mml:mo>&times;</mml:mo>\n<mml:msup>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>10</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mn>15</mml:mn>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msup>\n<mml:msub>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mi>M</mml:mi>\n</mml:mrow>\n<mml:mrow>\n<mml:mo>&CircleDot;</mml:mo>\n</mml:mrow>\n</mml:msub>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn5.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>) that was measured as a part of a weak lensing analysis of 27 clusters undertaken in the &ldquo;Weighing the Giants&rdquo; census (Applegate et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2\">2014</xref>).</p>\n<p>Based on all of these factors, MACS J0417 was included in the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS). RELICS is a large <italic>HST</italic> Treasury program, GO&nbsp;14096 (PI: Coe), to observe 46 fields strongly lensed by 41 massive galaxy clusters. The primary goals of the program are to identify candidates of high-redshift (6 &lt; <italic>z</italic> &lt; 12) galaxies magnified by the foreground clusters (Salmon et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib60\">2017</xref>, <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib61\">2018</xref>) with photometric redshifts estimated from multiband imaging with <italic>HST</italic> and <italic>Spitzer</italic> (PI: Brada&ccaron;), and to better constrain luminosity functions at the epoch of reionization. Full details of the project will be described in a forthcoming publication (D. Coe et al. 2019, in preparation). Of particular interest is the potential to identify targets to be observed with the <italic>James Webb Space Telescope</italic> (<italic>JWST</italic>). To support this goal and increase the scientific impact of this program, strong lens models are being computed by the RELICS team (Cerny et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib15\">2018</xref>; Acebron et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2a\">2018a</xref>, <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib2b\">2018b</xref>; Cibirka et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib17\">2018</xref>; Paterno-Mahler et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib53\">2018</xref>; Salmon et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib61\">2018</xref>) and released to the scientific community via the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes (MAST).<xref ref-type=\"fn\" rid=\"apjab042bfn1\"><sup>22</sup></xref>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bfn1\">\n<label><sup>22</sup></label>\n<p>\n<ext-link ext-link-type=\"uri\" xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"https://archive.stsci.edu/prepds/relics/\">https://archive.stsci.edu/prepds/relics/</ext-link>\n</p>\n</fn> The work presented here and in the companion paper, Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>), represent the first public strong lensing analyses on MACS J0417.5&minus;1154.</p>\n<p>The paper is organized as follows: In Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs2\">2</xref> we give an overview of the data. Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs3\">3</xref> details the strong lensing analysis, and the results are discussed in Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs4\">4</xref>. In Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs5\">5</xref> we describe predictions for observing the high-redshift universe by current and future facilities. In Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs6\">6</xref> we summarize the main results of this work.</p>\n<p>Throughout this paper we adopt a standard &Lambda;-CDM cosmology with &OHgr;<sub><italic>m</italic></sub>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;0.3, &OHgr;<sub>&Lambda;</sub>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;0.7 and <italic>h</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;0.7. All magnitudes are given in the AB system (Oke <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib51\">1974</xref>).</p>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2\">\n<label>2.</label>\n<title>Data</title>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2-1\">\n<label>2.1.</label>\n<title>Imaging</title>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2-1-1\">\n<label>2.1.1.</label>\n<title><italic>HST</italic></title>\n<p>MACS J0417 was first observed by <italic>HST</italic> in Cycle&nbsp;16, as part of a snapshot survey of the MACS clusters (SNAP 11103; PI: Ebeling) with the <italic>Wide Field Planetary Camera 2</italic> (WFPC2) in the F606W and F814W bands. Deeper observations with the UVIS instrument on the <italic>Wide Field Camera 3</italic> (WFC3) in the F606W band and with the <italic>Advanced Camera for Survey</italic> (ACS) in the F814W band were obtained in Cycle&nbsp;17 as part of the <italic>Chandra</italic> proposal ID #11800792 (joint with <italic>HST</italic> GO-12009; PI: von der Linden). It was then observed as part of the RELICS GO program with four filters on the WFC3-IR camera, F160W, F140W, F125W, and F105W; and F435W on ACS. Our analysis makes use of <italic>HST</italic> ACS and WFC3 imaging of MACS J0417, not the original WFPC2 shallow observations. Table <xref ref-type=\"table\" rid=\"apjab042bt1\">1</xref> lists the dates and exposure times of the <italic>HST</italic> observations used in this work.</p>\n<table-wrap id=\"apjab042bt1\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Table 1</label>\n<caption id=\"apjab042btc1\">\n<p>Details on the Observations of MACS J0417 Taken with the <italic>Hubble Space Telescope</italic></p>\n</caption>\n<table>\n<colgroup>\n<col align=\"left\"/>\n<col align=\"left\"/>\n<col align=\"center\"/>\n<col align=\"left\"/>\n</colgroup>\n<thead>\n<tr>\n<th align=\"left\">Camera, filter</th>\n<th align=\"left\">Exp. align=\"left\">GO-14096</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<graphic xlink:href=\"apjab042bt1_tb.gif\"/>\n<graphic xlink:href=\"apjab042bt1_lr.gif\">\n<caption>\n<p>Typeset image</p>\n</caption>\n</graphic>\n</table-wrap>\n<p>The ACS and WFC3 data were aligned to the same pixel frame and combined using standard procedures as described in Cerny et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib15\">2018</xref>). This work made use of images drizzled onto both 30 and 60 mas px<sup>&minus;1</sup>, to take advantage of the full resolution capabilities of the WFC3/UVIS and ACS cameras, and proper sampling of the point-spread function. We provide fully reduced imaging data as service to the community, and they are publicly available as high-level data products on MAST.</p>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2-1-2\">\n<label>2.1.2.</label>\n<title><italic>Spitzer</italic></title>\n<p>The IRAC on board the <italic>Spitzer Space Telescope</italic> imaged MACSJ 0417 as part of the S-RELICS programme (<italic>Spitzer</italic>-RELICS, PI: Brada&ccaron; PI: Soifer). Observations reach 13 hr of total exposure time in IRAC channels 1 and 2 (3.6 and 4.5 <italic>&mgr;</italic>m). The data reduction will be described in detail in V. Strait et al. (2019, in preparation); to create the mosaic images we use the <sc>MOsaicker and Point source EXtractor</sc> (<sc>mopex</sc><xref ref-type=\"fn\" rid=\"apjab042bfn2\"><sup>23</sup></xref>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bfn2\">\n<label><sup>23</sup></label>\n<p>\n<ext-link ext-link-type=\"uri\" xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"http://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/data/SPITZER/docs/dataanalysistools/tools/mopex/\">http://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/data/SPITZER/docs/dataanalysistools/tools/mopex/</ext-link>\n</p>\n</fn>) and largely follow the process described in the IRAC Cookbook<xref ref-type=\"fn\" rid=\"apjab042bfn3\"><sup>24</sup></xref>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bfn3\">\n<label><sup>24</sup></label>\n<p>\n<ext-link ext-link-type=\"uri\" xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"http://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/data/SPITZER/docs/dataanalysistools/cookbook/\">http://irsa.ipac.caltech.edu/data/SPITZER/docs/dataanalysistools/cookbook/</ext-link>\n</p>\n</fn> for the COSMOS medium-deep data.</p>\n<p>The intracluster light subtraction and flux extraction are done using <sc>t-phot</sc> (Merlin et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib46\">2015</xref>), designed to perform PSF-matched, prior-based, multiwavelength photometry as described in Merlin et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib46\">2015</xref>, <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib47\">2016</xref>). This is done by convolving cutouts from a high resolution image (in this case, F160W) using a low-resolution PSF transformation kernel that matches the F160W resolution to the IRAC (low-resolution) image. <sc>t-phot</sc> then fits a template to each source detected in F160W to best match the pixel values in the IRAC image. The IRAC fluxes are then combined with <italic>HST</italic> fluxes in catalogs.</p>\n</sec>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2-2\">\n<label>2.2.</label>\n<title>Spectroscopy</title>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2-2-1\">\n<label>2.2.1.</label>\n<title>LDSS3</title>\n<p>We obtained multislit spectroscopy of MACS J0417 with the upgraded Low Dispersion Survey Spectrograph (LDSS3-C)<xref ref-type=\"fn\" rid=\"apjab042bfn4\"><sup>25</sup></xref>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bfn4\">\n<label><sup>25</sup></label>\n<p>\n<ext-link ext-link-type=\"uri\" xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"http://www.lco.cl/telescopes-information/magellan/instruments/ldss-3\">http://www.lco.cl/telescopes-information/magellan/instruments/ldss-3</ext-link>\n</p>\n</fn> on the Magellan Clay telescope, on 2017 July 27 using the University of Michigan allocation (PI: Sharon). Two multislit masks were designed, with 1&farcs;0 slits placed on multiple images of lensed galaxies at the highest priority, and the rest of the mask filled with background sources and cluster-member galaxies. Due to weather conditions, only one of the masks was observed, with three exposures of 1200 s each. The seeing ranged between 0&farcs;5 and 0&farcs;7, with some clouds present during the observation. The data were obtained with the VPH-ALL grism (<inline-formula>\n<tex-math>\n<?CDATA $4250\\,\\mathring{\\rm A} \\lt \\lambda \\lt 10000\\,\\mathring{\\rm A} $?>\n</tex-math>\n<!-- <mml:math>\n<mml:mn>4250</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>&angst;</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo>&lt;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mi>&lambda;</mml:mi>\n<mml:mo>&lt;</mml:mo>\n<mml:mn>10000</mml:mn>\n<mml:mspace width=\"0.25em\"/>\n<mml:mi>&angst;</mml:mi>\n</mml:math> -->\n<inline-graphic xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" xlink:href=\"apjab042bieqn6.gif\"/>\n</inline-formula>) with spectral resolution <italic>R</italic>&nbsp;&equals;450&ndash;1100 across the wavelength range. The spectroscopic data were reduced using the standard procedures using the COSMOS data reduction package (Dressler et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib21\">2011</xref>; Oemler et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib48\">2017</xref>). We measured a spectroscopic redshift of <italic>z</italic><sub>spec</sub>&nbsp;&equals;0.871 for image 1.3 (<italic>&agr;</italic> &equals; 04:17:33.70, <italic>&delta;</italic> &equals; &minus;11:54:39.70), based on [O <sc>ii</sc>] <italic>&lambda;</italic> 3728 and H<italic>&beta;</italic> line emission. The data yielded a spectroscopic redshift for another background source (<italic>&agr;</italic> &equals; 4:17:35.942, <italic>&delta;</italic> &equals; &minus;11:54:59.29), at <italic>z</italic><sub>spec</sub>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;1.046, from [O <sc>ii</sc>] <italic>&lambda;</italic>3728, [O <sc>iii</sc>] <italic>&lambda;</italic>4959,5007; however, this source is not multiply imaged and was not used as a constraint in the lens model (see Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs3\">3</xref>).</p>\n<p>A full description of the RELICS Magellan/LDSS3 follow-up results will be presented in a future paper (R. Mainali et al. 2019, in preparation).</p>\n</sec>\n<sec id=\"apjab042bs2-2-2\">\n<label>2.2.2.</label>\n<title><italic>MUSE</italic></title>\n<p>The field was observed with the <italic>Multi Unit Spectrographic Explorer</italic> (<italic>MUSE</italic>; Bacon et al. <xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib5\">2010</xref>) on 2017 December 12. The <italic>MUSE</italic> exposure was 3&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;970 s, or 2910 s in total, and was taken as part of ESO project 0100.A-0792(A) (PI: Edge). The data were reduced and spectra extracted as explained in the companion paper Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>). The <italic>MUSE</italic> field of view (FOV), 1&prime;&nbsp;&times;&nbsp;1&prime;, is approximately centered on the BCG, and does not cover the full extent of the <italic>HST</italic> FOV. The <italic>MUSE</italic> spectral resolution is <italic>R</italic>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;1750&ndash;3750 across the wavelength range 4800&ndash;9300 &angst;.</p>\n<p>This work makes use of the spectroscopic redshifts measured for lensed galaxies reported in the companion paper by Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>) (Table <xref ref-type=\"table\" rid=\"apjab042bt2\">2</xref>). The <italic>MUSE</italic> observation confirms the redshift that was obtained with LDSS3 for image 1.3, <italic>z</italic><sub>spec</sub>&nbsp;&equals;0.871, and spectroscopically confirms images 1.1 and 1.2 as counter images of the same system. Moreover, it reveals [O <sc>ii</sc>] <italic>&lambda;</italic>3728 emission from a fourth image at the same redshift, buried in the light of the BCG. This fourth image is likely not a complete image; therefore, we did not use it as a constraint to model the cluster. The redshifts of system&nbsp;2 and system&nbsp;3 are both measured at <italic>z</italic><sub>spec</sub>&nbsp;&equals;&nbsp;1.046. The two systems correspond to two different galaxies separated by &sim;140 kpc in the source plane according to our modeling.</p>\n<table-wrap id=\"apjab042bt2\" position=\"float\">\n<label>Table <italic>z</italic><sub>spec</sub> refers to the spectroscopic constraints when available; references for the spectroscopic redshifts are given in the table footnotes. <italic>z</italic><sub>model</sub> indicates the best-fit redshift estimates resulting from the &ldquo;silver&rdquo; and &ldquo;bronze&rdquo; lens models with their respective statistical uncertainties. The rms is the difference between the observed position of a multiple image and the predicted position from the barycenter of our best-fit model in the image plane given in arcseconds. The classification scheme is discussed in Section <xref ref-type=\"sec\" rid=\"apjab042bs3-2\">3.2</xref>.</p>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bt1fna\">\n<label><sup>a</sup></label>\n<p>Spectroscopic redshift from Magellan/LDSS3 (this work) and confirmed by <italic>MUSE</italic> in Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>).</p>\n</fn>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bt1fnb\">\n<label><sup>b</sup></label>\n<p>Spectroscopic redshifts from <italic>MUSE</italic> presented in Jauzac et al. (<xref ref-type=\"bibr\" rid=\"apjab042bbib3a\">2019</xref>). Sources 2 and 3 are at the same redshift. These galaxies are separated by &sim;140 kpc in the source plane.</p>\n</fn>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bt1fnc\">\n<label><sup>c</sup></label>\n<p>In system 10, images 10.1 and 10.2 are classified as <italic>silver</italic> and 10.3 is classified as <italic>bronze</italic>. Image 10.3 was therefore not included in the &ldquo;silver&rdquo; model.</p>\n</fn>\n<fn id=\"apjab042bt1fnd\">\n<label><sup>d</sup></label>\n<p>These redshifts and rms values are computed using the best-fit model computed with <italic>silver</italic> constraints fixed, while only the redshifts of those systems are being optimized.</p>\n</fn>\n</table-wrap-foot>\n</table-wrap>\n<table-wrap
